	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">United States House of Representatives</h1>

<a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_United_States.svg" class="image"><img alt="Flag of the United States.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/32px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png" decoding="async" width="32" height="17" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/48px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/64px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1235" data-file-height="650" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:United_States" title="Portal:United States">United States&#32;portal</a> <div class="hlist" style="text-align:center;font-weight:normal;">

</div></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Politics_of_the_United_States" title="Template:Politics of the United States"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Politics_of_the_United_States" title="Template talk:Politics of the United States"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Politics_of_the_United_States&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>United States House of Representatives</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Lower_house" title="Lower house">lower house</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senate</a> being the <a href="/wiki/Upper_house" title="Upper house">upper house</a>. Together they compose the national <a href="/wiki/Legislature" title="Legislature">legislature</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>.
</p><p>The composition of the House is established by <a href="/wiki/Article_One_of_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Article One of the United States Constitution">Article One of the United States Constitution</a>. The House is composed of representatives who sit in <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_congressional_districts" title="List of United States congressional districts">congressional districts</a> that are allocated to each of the 50 states on a basis of population as measured by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census" title="United States Census">U.S. Census</a>, with each district entitled to one representative. Since its inception in 1789, all representatives have been directly elected. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_congressional_apportionment" title="United States congressional apportionment">total number of voting representatives</a> is <a href="/wiki/Apportionment_Act_of_1911" title="Apportionment Act of 1911">fixed by law</a> at 435.<sup id="cite_ref-USPL_62-5_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USPL_62-5-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> As of the <a href="/wiki/2010_United_States_Census" title="2010 United States Census">2010 Census</a>, the largest delegation is that of <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Representatives_from_California" title="List of United States Representatives from California">California</a>, with fifty-three representatives. Seven states have <a href="/wiki/At-large" title="At-large">only one representative</a>: <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delaware" title="Delaware">Delaware</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">Montana</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">North Dakota</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Dakota" title="South Dakota">South Dakota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The House is charged with the passage of federal <a href="/wiki/Legislation" title="Legislation">legislation</a>, known as <a href="/wiki/Bill_(United_States_Congress)" title="Bill (United States Congress)">bills</a>, which, after concurrence by the Senate, are sent to the <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">president</a> for consideration. In addition to this basic power, the House has certain exclusive powers, among them the power to initiate all bills related to revenue; the <a href="/wiki/Impeachment_in_the_United_States" title="Impeachment in the United States">impeachment</a> of federal officers, who are sent to trial before the Senate; and, in cases wherein no candidate receives a majority of <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="Electoral College (United States)">electors</a> for <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">president</a>, the duty falls upon the House to elect one of the top three recipients of electors for that office, with one vote given to each state for that purpose.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The House meets in the south wing of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">United States Capitol</a>.
</p><p>The presiding officer is the <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the United States House of Representatives">speaker of the House</a>, who is elected by the members thereof (and is therefore traditionally the leader of the controlling <a href="/wiki/Political_parties_in_the_United_States" title="Political parties in the United States">party</a>). The speaker and <a href="/wiki/Party_leaders_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Party leaders of the United States House of Representatives">other floor leaders</a> are chosen by the <a href="/wiki/House_Democratic_Caucus" title="House Democratic Caucus">Democratic Caucus</a> or the <a href="/wiki/House_Republican_Caucus" class="mw-redirect" title="House Republican Caucus">Republican Conference</a>, depending on whichever <a href="/wiki/Party_caucuses_and_conferences_in_the_United_States_Congress" title="Party caucuses and conferences in the United States Congress">party</a> has more voting members.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="History of the United States House of Representatives">History of the United States House of Representatives</a></div>
<p>Under the <a href="/wiki/Articles_of_Confederation" title="Articles of Confederation">Articles of Confederation</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_Confederation" title="Congress of the Confederation">Congress of the Confederation</a> was a <a href="/wiki/Unicameralism" title="Unicameralism">unicameral</a> body in which each state was equally represented, and in which each state had a veto over most action. After eight years of a more limited <a href="/wiki/Confederation" title="Confederation">confederal</a> government under the Articles, numerous political leaders such as <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a> initiated the <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Convention_(United_States)" title="Constitutional Convention (United States)">Constitutional Convention</a> in 1787, which received the Confederation Congress's sanction to "amend the Articles of Confederation". All states except <a href="/wiki/Rhode_Island" title="Rhode Island">Rhode Island</a> agreed to send delegates.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Historical_distribution_of_parties_in_US_House_of_Representatives.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Historical_distribution_of_parties_in_US_House_of_Representatives.svg/220px-Historical_distribution_of_parties_in_US_House_of_Representatives.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Historical_distribution_of_parties_in_US_House_of_Representatives.svg/330px-Historical_distribution_of_parties_in_US_House_of_Representatives.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Historical_distribution_of_parties_in_US_House_of_Representatives.svg/440px-Historical_distribution_of_parties_in_US_House_of_Representatives.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="5487" data-file-height="3093" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Historical_distribution_of_parties_in_US_House_of_Representatives.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Representation of all political parties as percentage in House of Representatives over time</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Combined--Control_of_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_-_Control_of_the_U.S._Senate.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Combined--Control_of_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_-_Control_of_the_U.S._Senate.png/220px-Combined--Control_of_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_-_Control_of_the_U.S._Senate.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="108" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Combined--Control_of_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_-_Control_of_the_U.S._Senate.png/330px-Combined--Control_of_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_-_Control_of_the_U.S._Senate.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Combined--Control_of_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_-_Control_of_the_U.S._Senate.png/440px-Combined--Control_of_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_-_Control_of_the_U.S._Senate.png 2x" data-file-width="1442" data-file-height="706" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Combined--Control_of_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_-_Control_of_the_U.S._Senate.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Historical graph of party control of the Senate and House as well as the presidency<sup id="cite_ref-uspolitics.about_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uspolitics.about-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The issue of how to structure Congress was one of the most divisive among the <a href="/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States" title="Founding Fathers of the United States">founders</a> during the Convention. <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Randolph" title="Edmund Randolph">Edmund Randolph</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Plan" title="Virginia Plan">Virginia Plan</a> called for a <a href="/wiki/Bicameralism" title="Bicameralism">bicameral</a> Congress: the lower house would be "of the people", elected directly by the people of the United States and representing <a href="/wiki/Public_opinion" title="Public opinion">public opinion</a>, and a more deliberative upper house, elected by the lower house, that would represent the individual states, and would be less susceptible to variations of mass sentiment.<sup id="cite_ref-Plans_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plans-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The House is referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Lower_house" title="Lower house">lower house</a>, with the Senate being the <a href="/wiki/Upper_house" title="Upper house">upper house</a>, although the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a> does not use that terminology. Both houses' approval is necessary for the passage of <a href="/wiki/Legislation" title="Legislation">legislation</a>. The Virginia Plan drew the support of delegates from large states such as <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>, as it called for representation based on population. The smaller states, however, favored the <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey_Plan" title="New Jersey Plan">New Jersey Plan</a>, which called for a unicameral Congress with equal representation for the states.<sup id="cite_ref-Plans_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plans-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Eventually, the Convention reached the <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_Compromise" title="Connecticut Compromise">Connecticut Compromise</a> or Great Compromise, under which one house of Congress (the House of Representatives) would provide representation proportional to each state's population, whereas the other (the Senate) would provide equal representation amongst the states.<sup id="cite_ref-Plans_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plans-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The Constitution was ratified by the requisite number of states (nine out of the 13) in 1788, but its implementation was set for March 4, 1789. The House began work on April 1, 1789, when it achieved a <a href="/wiki/Quorum" title="Quorum">quorum</a> for the first time.
</p><p>During the first half of the 19th century, the House was frequently in conflict with the Senate over regionally divisive issues, including <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States" title="Slavery in the United States">slavery</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_United_States" title="Northeastern United States">North</a> was much more populous than the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Southern_United_States" title="History of the Southern United States">South</a>, and therefore dominated the House of Representatives. However, the North held no such advantage in the Senate, where the equal representation of states prevailed.
</p><p>Regional conflict was most pronounced over the issue of slavery. One example of a provision repeatedly supported by the House but blocked by the Senate was the <a href="/wiki/Wilmot_Proviso" title="Wilmot Proviso">Wilmot Proviso</a>, which sought to ban slavery in the land gained during the <a href="/wiki/Mexican%E2%80%93American_War" title="MexicanAmerican War">MexicanAmerican War</a>. Conflict over slavery and other issues persisted until the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a> (18611865), which began soon after several southern states attempted to <a href="/wiki/Secession" title="Secession">secede</a> from the Union. The war culminated in the South's defeat and in the abolition of slavery. All southern senators except <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Johnson" title="Andrew Johnson">Andrew Johnson</a> resigned their seats at the beginning of the war, and therefore the Senate did not hold the balance of power between North and South during the war.
</p><p>The years of <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era" class="mw-redirect" title="Reconstruction Era">Reconstruction</a> that followed witnessed large majorities for the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States_Republican_Party" title="History of the United States Republican Party">Republican Party</a>, which many Americans associated with the Union's victory in the Civil War and the ending of slavery. The Reconstruction period ended in about 1877; the ensuing era, known as the <a href="/wiki/Gilded_Age" title="Gilded Age">Gilded Age</a>, was marked by sharp political divisions in the electorate. The <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States_Democratic_Party" title="History of the United States Democratic Party">Democratic Party</a> and Republican Party each held majorities in the House at various times.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Brackett_Reed_-_Brady-Handy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Thomas_Brackett_Reed_-_Brady-Handy.jpg/200px-Thomas_Brackett_Reed_-_Brady-Handy.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="259" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Thomas_Brackett_Reed_-_Brady-Handy.jpg/300px-Thomas_Brackett_Reed_-_Brady-Handy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Thomas_Brackett_Reed_-_Brady-Handy.jpg/400px-Thomas_Brackett_Reed_-_Brady-Handy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1297" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Brackett_Reed_-_Brady-Handy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the United States House of Representatives">speaker of the House</a> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Brackett_Reed" title="Thomas Brackett Reed">Thomas Brackett Reed</a> (18951899)</div></div></div>
<p>The late 19th and early 20th centuries also saw a dramatic increase in the power of the <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the United States House of Representatives">speaker of the House</a>. The rise of the speaker's influence began in the 1890s, during the tenure of Republican <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Brackett_Reed" title="Thomas Brackett Reed">Thomas Brackett Reed</a>. "<a href="/wiki/Czar" class="mw-redirect" title="Czar">Czar</a> Reed", as he was nicknamed, attempted to put into effect his view that "The best system is to have one party govern and the other party watch." The leadership structure of the House also developed during approximately the same period, with the positions of <a href="/wiki/Majority_Leader_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" class="mw-redirect" title="Majority Leader of the United States House of Representatives">majority leader</a> and <a href="/wiki/Minority_leader_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" class="mw-redirect" title="Minority leader of the United States House of Representatives">minority leader</a> being created in 1899. While the minority leader was the head of the minority party, the majority leader remained subordinate to the speaker. The speakership reached its zenith during the term of Republican <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Gurney_Cannon" title="Joseph Gurney Cannon">Joseph Gurney Cannon</a>, from 1903 to 1911. The powers of the speaker included chairmanship of the influential <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Rules" title="United States House Committee on Rules">Rules Committee</a> and the ability to appoint members of other House committees. These powers, however, were curtailed in the "Revolution of 1910" because of the efforts of Democrats and dissatisfied Republicans who opposed Cannon's arguably heavy-handed tactics.
</p><p>The Democratic Party dominated the House of Representatives during the administration of President <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> (19331945), often winning over two-thirds of the seats. Both Democrats and Republicans were in power at various times during the next decade. The Democratic Party maintained control of the House from 1955 until 1995. In the mid-1970s, there were major reforms of the House, strengthening the power of sub-committees at the expense of committee chairs and allowing party leaders to nominate committee chairs. These actions were taken to undermine the <a href="/wiki/Seniority_in_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Seniority in the United States House of Representatives">seniority system</a>, and to reduce the ability of a small number of senior members to obstruct legislation they did not favor. There was also a shift from the 1990s to greater control of the legislative program by the majority party; the power of party leaders (especially the speaker) grew considerably.
</p><p>The Republicans <a href="/wiki/Republican_Revolution" title="Republican Revolution">took control of the House</a> in 1995, under the leadership of Speaker <a href="/wiki/Newt_Gingrich" title="Newt Gingrich">Newt Gingrich</a>. Gingrich attempted to pass a major legislative program, the <a href="/wiki/Contract_with_America" title="Contract with America">Contract with America</a>, on which the House Republicans had been elected, and made major reforms of the House, notably reducing the tenure of committee chairs to three two-year terms. Many elements of the Contract did not pass Congress, were vetoed by President <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a>, or were substantially altered in negotiations with Clinton. However, after Republicans held control in the 1996 election, Clinton and the Gingrich-led House agreed on the first balanced federal budget in decades, along with a substantial tax cut.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The Republicans held on to the House until 2006, when the Democrats won control and <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Pelosi" title="Nancy Pelosi">Nancy Pelosi</a> was subsequently elected by the House as the first female speaker. The Republicans retook the House in 2011, with the largest shift of power since the 1930s.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> However, the Democrats retook the house 8 years later in 2019, which became the largest shift of power to the Democrats since the 1970s.
</p>
<h2><span id="Membership.2C_qualifications.2C_and_apportionment"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Membership,_qualifications,_and_apportionment">Membership, qualifications, and apportionment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Membership, qualifications, and apportionment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks plainlist" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%;width:18em;"><tbody><tr><td style="padding-top:0.4em;line-height:1.2em">This article is part of <a href="/wiki/Category:United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Category:United States House of Representatives">a series</a> on the</td></tr><tr><th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;padding-top:0;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em;font-size:175%;font-weight:normal;"><a class="mw-selflink selflink">United States House<br />of Representatives</a></th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0.2em 0 0.4em"><div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:Seal_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives.svg" class="image" title="Great Seal of the United States House of Representatives"><img alt="Great Seal of the United States House of Representatives" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Seal_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives.svg/75px-Seal_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives.svg.png" decoding="async" width="75" height="75" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Seal_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives.svg/113px-Seal_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Seal_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives.svg/150px-Seal_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1030" data-file-height="1030" /></a></div></td></tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold">
