<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Apportionments">Apportionments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Apportionments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_congressional_apportionment" title="United States congressional apportionment">United States congressional apportionment</a></div>
<p>Under <a href="/wiki/Article_One_of_the_United_States_Constitution#House_of_Representatives" title="Article One of the United States Constitution">Article I, Section 2 of the Constitution</a>, seats in the House of Representatives are <a href="/wiki/United_States_congressional_apportionment" title="United States congressional apportionment">apportioned</a> among the states by population, as determined by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census" title="United States Census">census</a> conducted every ten years. Each state is entitled to at least one representative, however small its population.
</p><p>The only constitutional rule relating to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_congressional_apportionment" title="United States congressional apportionment">size of the House</a> states: "The Number of Representatives shall not exceed one for every thirty Thousand, but each State shall have at Least one Representative."<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Congress regularly increased the size of the House to account for population growth until it fixed the number of voting House members at 435 in 1911.<sup id="cite_ref-USPL_62-5_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USPL_62-5-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> In 1959, upon the admission of <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>, the number was temporarily increased to 437 (seating one representative from each of those states without changing existing apportionment), and returned to 435 four years later, after the reapportionment consequent to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census,_1960" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Census, 1960">1960 census</a>.
</p><p>The Constitution does not provide for the representation of the <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">District of Columbia</a> or of <a href="/wiki/United_States_territory" title="United States territory">territories</a>. The <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia_voting_rights" title="District of Columbia voting rights">District of Columbia</a> and the territories of <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>, <a href="/wiki/American_Samoa" title="American Samoa">American Samoa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">Guam</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Mariana_Islands" title="Northern Mariana Islands">Northern Mariana Islands</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Virgin_Islands" title="United States Virgin Islands">U.S. Virgin Islands</a> are each represented by one <a href="/wiki/Delegate_(United_States_Congress)" class="mw-redirect" title="Delegate (United States Congress)">non-voting delegate</a>. Puerto Rico elects a <a href="/wiki/Resident_Commissioner_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Resident Commissioner of Puerto Rico">resident commissioner</a>, but other than having a four-year term, the resident commissioner's role is identical to the delegates from the other territories. The five delegates and resident commissioner may participate in debates; prior to 2011,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> they were also allowed to vote in committees and the <a href="/wiki/Committee_of_the_Whole_(United_States_House_of_Representatives)" title="Committee of the Whole (United States House of Representatives)">Committee of the Whole</a> when their votes would not be decisive.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Redistricting">Redistricting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Redistricting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Redistricting" title="Redistricting">Redistricting</a></div>
<p>States that are entitled to more than one Representative are divided into <a href="/wiki/Single-member_district" title="Single-member district">single-member</a> <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_congressional_districts" title="List of United States congressional districts">districts</a>. This has been a federal statutory requirement since 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Prior to that law, <a href="/wiki/General_ticket" title="General ticket">general ticket</a> representation was used by some states.
</p><p>States typically <a href="/wiki/Redistricting" title="Redistricting">redraw district boundaries</a> after each census, though they may do so at other times, such as the <a href="/wiki/2003_Texas_redistricting" title="2003 Texas redistricting">2003 Texas redistricting</a>. Each state determines its own district boundaries, either through legislation or through non-partisan panels. "<a href="/wiki/Malapportionment" class="mw-redirect" title="Malapportionment">Malapportionment</a>" is unconstitutional and districts must be approximately equal in population (see <i><a href="/wiki/Wesberry_v._Sanders" title="Wesberry v. Sanders">Wesberry v. Sanders</a></i>). Additionally, Section 2 of the <a href="/wiki/Voting_Rights_Act_of_1965" title="Voting Rights Act of 1965">Voting Rights Act of 1965</a> prohibits redistricting plans that are intended to, or have the effect of, discriminating against racial or language minority voters.<sup id="cite_ref-Section_2_Voting_Rights_Act_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Section_2_Voting_Rights_Act-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Aside from malapportionment and discrimination against racial or language minorities, federal courts have allowed state legislatures to engage in <a href="/wiki/Gerrymandering" title="Gerrymandering">gerrymandering</a> for the benefit of political parties or incumbents.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> In a 1984 case, <i><a href="/wiki/Davis_v._Bandemer" title="Davis v. Bandemer">Davis v. Bandemer</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court</a> held that gerrymandered districts could be struck down on the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Equal_Protection_Clause" title="Equal Protection Clause">Equal Protection Clause</a>, but the Court did not articulate a standard for when districts are impermissibly gerrymandered. However, the Court overruled Davis in 2004 in <i><a href="/wiki/Vieth_v._Jubelirer" title="Vieth v. Jubelirer">Vieth v. Jubelirer</a></i>, and Court precedent currently holds gerrymandering to be a <a href="/wiki/Political_question" title="Political question">political question</a>. According to calculations made by <a href="/wiki/Burt_Neuborne" title="Burt Neuborne">Burt Neuborne</a> using criteria set forth by the <a href="/wiki/American_Political_Science_Association" title="American Political Science Association">American Political Science Association</a>, about 40 seats, less than 10% of the House membership, are chosen through a genuinely contested electoral process, given partisan gerrymandering.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Qualifications">Qualifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Qualifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Article I, Section 2 of the Constitution sets three qualifications for representatives. Each representative must: (1) be at least twenty-five years old; (2) have been a <a href="/wiki/United_States_nationality_law" title="United States nationality law">citizen</a> of the United States for the past seven years; and (3) be (at the time of the election) an inhabitant of the state they represent. Members are not required to live in the districts they represent, but they traditionally do.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The age and citizenship qualifications for representatives are less than those for senators. The constitutional requirements of Article I, Section 2 for election to Congress are the maximum requirements that can be imposed on a candidate.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Therefore, Article I, Section 5, which permits each House to be the judge of the qualifications of its own members does not permit either House to establish additional qualifications. Likewise a State could not establish additional qualifications.
</p><p>Disqualification: under the <a href="/wiki/Fourteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Fourteenth Amendment</a>, a federal or state officer who takes the requisite oath to support the Constitution, but later engages in rebellion or aids the enemies of the United States, is disqualified from becoming a representative. This postCivil War provision was intended to prevent those who sided with the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederacy</a> from serving. However, disqualified individuals may serve if they gain the consent of two-thirds of both houses of Congress.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Elections">Elections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Elections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HouseDelegations2013-23.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/HouseDelegations2013-23.svg/300px-HouseDelegations2013-23.svg.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/HouseDelegations2013-23.svg/450px-HouseDelegations2013-23.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/HouseDelegations2013-23.svg/600px-HouseDelegations2013-23.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="659" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HouseDelegations2013-23.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>All 435 voting seats of the current House shown grouped by state, largest to smallest</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pop_per_rep.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/7d/Pop_per_rep.png/300px-Pop_per_rep.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/7d/Pop_per_rep.png/450px-Pop_per_rep.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/7d/Pop_per_rep.png/600px-Pop_per_rep.png 2x" data-file-width="620" data-file-height="343" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pop_per_rep.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Population per U.S. representative allocated to each of the 50 states and D.C., ranked by population. Since D.C. (ranked 50th) receives no voting seats in the House, its bar is absent.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:115th_United_States_Congress_Congressional_Districts.pdf" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/115th_United_States_Congress_Congressional_Districts.pdf/page1-300px-115th_United_States_Congress_Congressional_Districts.pdf.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="222" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/115th_United_States_Congress_Congressional_Districts.pdf/page1-450px-115th_United_States_Congress_Congressional_Districts.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/115th_United_States_Congress_Congressional_Districts.pdf/page1-600px-115th_United_States_Congress_Congressional_Districts.pdf.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6675" data-file-height="4950" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:115th_United_States_Congress_Congressional_Districts.pdf" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>U.S. congressional districts for the 115th Congress</div></div></div>
<p>Elections for representatives are held in every even-numbered year, on <a href="/wiki/Election_Day_(United_States)" title="Election Day (United States)">Election Day</a> the first Tuesday after the first Monday in November. By law, representatives must be elected from <a href="/wiki/Single-member_districts" class="mw-redirect" title="Single-member districts">single-member districts</a>. After a census is taken (in a year ending in 0), the year ending in 2 is the first year in which elections for U.S. House districts are based on that census (with the Congress based on those districts starting its term on the following Jan. 3).
</p><p>In most states, major party candidates for each district are nominated in partisan <a href="/wiki/Primary_election" title="Primary election">primary elections</a>, typically held in spring to late summer. In some states, the Republican and Democratic parties choose their respective candidates for each district in their <a href="/wiki/Political_convention" title="Political convention">political conventions</a> in spring or early summer, which often use unanimous voice votes to reflect either confidence in the incumbent or the result of bargaining in earlier private discussions. Exceptions can result in so-called floor fightconvention votes by delegates, with outcomes that can be hard to predict. Especially if a convention is closely divided, a losing candidate may contend further by meeting the conditions for a primary election.
</p><p>The courts generally do not consider <a href="/wiki/Ballot_access" title="Ballot access">ballot access</a> rules for <a href="/wiki/Independent_politician" title="Independent politician">independent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Third_party_(politics)" title="Third party (politics)">third party</a> candidates to be additional qualifications for holding office and there are no federal regulations regarding ballot access. As a result, the process to gain ballot access varies greatly from state to state, and in the case of a <a href="/wiki/Third_party_(politics)" title="Third party (politics)">third party</a> may be affected by results of previous years' elections.
</p><p>In 1967, the United States Congress passed the Uniform Congressional District Act, which requires almost all representatives be elected from single-member-districts.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-schaller_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schaller-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Following the <i><a href="/wiki/Wesberry_v._Sanders" title="Wesberry v. Sanders">Wesberry v. Sanders</a></i> decision, Congress was motivated by fears that courts would impose <a href="/wiki/Plurality-at-large_voting" title="Plurality-at-large voting">at-large plurality districts</a> on states that did not redistrict to comply with the new mandates for districts roughly equal in population, and Congress also sought to prevent attempts by southern states to use such voting systems to dilute the vote of racial minorities.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Several states have used multi-member districts in the past, although only two states (Hawaii and New Mexico) used multi-member districts in 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-schaller_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schaller-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Hawaii and New Mexico were made exempt from the Uniform Congressional District Act, and are free to use multi-member districts, although neither state chooses to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-schaller_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schaller-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> is unique in that it holds an all-party "primary election" on the general Election Day with a subsequent <a href="/wiki/Two-round_system" title="Two-round system">run-off election</a> between the top two finishers (regardless of party) if no candidate received a majority in the primary. The states of <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a> and <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> now use a similar (though not identical) system to that used by Louisiana. Seats vacated during a term are filled through special elections, unless the vacancy occurs closer to the next general election date than a pre-established deadline. The term of a member chosen in a special election usually begins the next day, or as soon as the results are certified.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-voting_delegates">Non-voting delegates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Non-voting delegates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Non-voting_members_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Non-voting members of the United States House of Representatives">Non-voting members of the United States House of Representatives</a></div>
<p>Historically, many states, prior to achieving statehood, have sent <a href="/wiki/Non-voting_members_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Non-voting members of the United States House of Representatives">non-voting delegates</a> to the House. While their role has fluctuated over the years, today they have many of the same privileges as voting members, have a voice in committees, and can introduce bills on the floor, but cannot vote on the ultimate passage of bills. Presently, <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">the District of Columbia</a> and the five inhabited <a href="/wiki/Territories_of_the_United_States" title="Territories of the United States">U.S. territories</a> each elect a delegate. A seventh delegate, representing the <a href="/wiki/Cherokee_Nation" title="Cherokee Nation">Cherokee Nation</a>, has been formally proposed but has not yet been seated.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, some territories may choose to also elect <a href="/wiki/Shadow_congressperson" title="Shadow congressperson">shadow representatives</a>, though these are not official members of the House and are separate individuals from their official delegates.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Terms">Terms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Terms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Representatives and delegates serve for two-year terms, while a <a href="/wiki/Resident_commissioner#The_United_States_of_America" title="Resident commissioner">resident commissioner</a> (a kind of delegate) serves for four years. A term starts on January 3 following the election in November. The U.S. Constitution requires that vacancies in the House be filled with a special election. The term of the replacement member expires on the date that the original member's would have expired.
</p><p>The Constitution permits the House to expel a member with a two-thirds vote. In the history of the United States, only five members have been expelled from the House; in 1861, three were removed for supporting the Confederate states' secession: <a href="/wiki/John_Bullock_Clark" title="John Bullock Clark">John Bullock Clark</a> (D-MO), <a href="/wiki/John_William_Reid" title="John William Reid">John William Reid</a> (D-MO) and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cornelius_Burnett" title="Henry Cornelius Burnett">Henry Cornelius Burnett</a> (D-KY). <a href="/wiki/Michael_Myers_(politician)" title="Michael Myers (politician)">Michael Myers</a> (D-PA) was expelled after his criminal conviction for accepting bribes in 1980, and <a href="/wiki/James_Traficant" title="James Traficant">James Traficant</a> (D-OH) was expelled in 2002 following his conviction for corruption.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The House also has the power to formally <a href="/wiki/Censure" title="Censure">censure</a> or reprimand its members; censure or reprimand of a member requires only a simple majority, and does not remove that member from office.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_to_the_Senate">Comparison to the Senate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Comparison to the Senate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As a check on the regional, popular, and rapidly changing politics of the House, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senate</a> has several distinct powers. For example, the "<a href="/wiki/Advice_and_consent" title="Advice and consent">advice and consent</a>" powers (such as the power to approve <a href="/wiki/Treaty" title="Treaty">treaties</a> and confirm members of the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_States" title="Cabinet of the United States">Cabinet</a>) are a sole Senate privilege.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The House, however, has the exclusive power to initiate bills for raising revenue, to impeach officials, and to choose the <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">president</a> in the event that a presidential candidate fails to get a majority of the Electoral College votes.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The Senate and House are further differentiated by term lengths and the number of districts represented: the Senate has longer terms of six years, fewer members (currently one hundred, two for each state), and (in all but seven delegations) larger constituencies per member. The Senate is informally referred to as the "upper" house, and the House of Representatives as the "lower" house.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Salary_and_benefits">Salary and benefits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Salary and benefits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Salaries">Salaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Salaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As of December&#160;2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the annual salary of each representative is <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">$</a>174,000.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brudnick_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brudnick-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the United States House of Representatives">speaker of the House</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Majority_Leader_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" class="mw-redirect" title="Majority Leader of the United States House of Representatives">majority</a> and <a href="/wiki/Minority_leader_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" class="mw-redirect" title="Minority leader of the United States House of Representatives">minority leaders</a> earn more: $223,500 for the speaker and $193,400 for their party leaders (the same as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senate</a> leaders).<sup id="cite_ref-Brudnick_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brudnick-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Cost-of-living_index" title="Cost-of-living index">cost-of-living-adjustment</a> (COLA) increase takes effect annually unless Congress votes not to accept it. Congress sets members' salaries; however, the <a href="/wiki/Twenty-seventh_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twenty-seventh Amendment to the United States Constitution">Twenty-seventh Amendment to the United States Constitution</a> prohibits a change in salary (but not COLA<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>) from taking effect until after the next election of the whole House. Representatives are eligible for retirement benefits after serving for five years.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Outside pay is limited to 15% of congressional pay, and certain types of income involving a fiduciary responsibility or personal endorsement are prohibited. Salaries are not for life, only during active term.<sup id="cite_ref-Brudnick_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brudnick-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Titles">Titles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Titles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Representatives use the prefix "<a href="/wiki/The_Honorable" class="mw-redirect" title="The Honorable">The Honorable</a>" before their names. A member of the House is referred to as a <i>representative</i>, <i>congressman</i>, or <i>congresswoman</i>. While senators are members of Congress, the terms <i>congressman</i> and <i>congresswoman</i> are not generally used by them.
</p><p>Representatives are usually identified in the media and other sources by a major city or community within their congressional district instead of the district number, prefixed by the abbreviation of their party. For example, House Speaker <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Pelosi" title="Nancy Pelosi">Nancy Pelosi</a> is "DSan Francisco", meaning she is a Democrat who represents <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a>, rather than just being referred to as representing  <a href="/wiki/California%27s_12th_congressional_district" title="California&#39;s 12th congressional district">California's 12th congressional district</a>. Minority leader <a href="/wiki/Kevin_McCarthy_(California_politician)" title="Kevin McCarthy (California politician)">Kevin McCarthy</a> is "RBakersfield", a Republican representing <a href="/wiki/Bakersfield,_California" title="Bakersfield, California">Bakersfield, California</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pension">Pension</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Pension">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>All members of Congress are automatically (without the option of withdrawal) enrolled in the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Employees_Retirement_System" title="Federal Employees Retirement System">Federal Employees Retirement System</a>, a pension system also used for <a href="/wiki/United_States_federal_civil_service" title="United States federal civil service">federal civil servants</a>. They become eligible to receive benefits after five years of service (two and one-half terms in the House). The FERS is composed of three elements:
</p>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Social_Security_(United_States)" title="Social Security (United States)">Social Security</a></li>
<li>The FERS basic annuity, a monthly pension plan based on the number of years of service and the average of the three highest years of basic pay</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Thrift_Savings_Plan" title="Thrift Savings Plan">Thrift Savings Plan</a>, a <a href="/wiki/401(k)" title="401(k)">401(k)</a>-like <a href="/wiki/Defined_contribution_plan" title="Defined contribution plan">defined contribution plan</a> for retirement account into which participants can deposit up to a maximum of $19,000 in 2019. Their employing agency <a href="/wiki/Employer_Matching_Program" title="Employer Matching Program">matches employee contributions</a> up to 5% of pay.</li></ol>
<p>Members of Congress may retire with full benefits at age 62 after five years of service, at age 50 after twenty years of service, and at any age after twenty-five years of service. They may retire with reduced benefits at ages 55 to 59 after five years of service. Depending on birth year, they may receive a reduced pension after ten years of service if they are between 55 years and 57 years of age.<sup id="cite_ref-CRS_Retirement_Report_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRS_Retirement_Report-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tax_deductions">Tax deductions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Tax deductions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Members of Congress are permitted to deduct up to $3,000 of living expenses per year incurred while living away from their district or home state.<sup id="cite_ref-Congress_Allowances_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Congress_Allowances-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_benefits">Health benefits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Health benefits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Prior to 2014, members of Congress and their staff had access to essentially the same health benefits as federal civil servants; they could voluntarily enroll in the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Employees_Health_Benefits_Program" title="Federal Employees Health Benefits Program">Federal Employees Health Benefits Program</a> (FEHBP), an employer-sponsored health insurance program, and were eligible to participate in other programs, such as the Federal Flexible Spending Account Program (FSAFEDS).<sup id="cite_ref-Mach_&amp;_Cornell_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mach_&amp;_Cornell-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, Section 1312(d)(3)(D) of the <a href="/wiki/Patient_Protection_and_Affordable_Care_Act" title="Patient Protection and Affordable Care Act">Patient Protection and Affordable Care Act</a> (ACA) provided that the only health plans that the federal government can make available to members of Congress and certain congressional staff are those created under the ACA or offered through a <a href="/wiki/Health_insurance_marketplace" title="Health insurance marketplace">health care exchange</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Personnel_Management" class="mw-redirect" title="Office of Personnel Management">Office of Personnel Management</a> promulgated a final rule to comply with Section 1312(d)(3)(D).<sup id="cite_ref-Mach_&amp;_Cornell_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mach_&amp;_Cornell-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Under the rule, effective January 1, 2014, members and designated staff are no longer able to purchase FEHBP plans as active employees.<sup id="cite_ref-Mach_&amp;_Cornell_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mach_&amp;_Cornell-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> However, if members enroll in a health plan offered through a Small Business Health Options Program (SHOP) exchange, they remain eligible for an employer contribution toward coverage, and members and designated staff who are eligible for retirement may enroll in a FEHBP plan upon retirement.<sup id="cite_ref-Mach_&amp;_Cornell_33-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mach_&amp;_Cornell-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The ACA and the final rule do not affect members' or staffers' eligibility for <a href="/wiki/Medicare_(United_States)" title="Medicare (United States)">Medicare</a> benefits.<sup id="cite_ref-Mach_&amp;_Cornell_33-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mach_&amp;_Cornell-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The ACA and the final rule also do not affect members' and staffers' eligibility for other health benefits related to federal employment, so current members and staff are eligible to participate in FSAFEDS (which has three options within the program), the Federal Employees Dental and Vision Insurance Program, and the Federal Long Term Care Insurance Program.<sup id="cite_ref-Mach_&amp;_Cornell_33-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mach_&amp;_Cornell-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There is an <a href="/wiki/Attending_Physician_of_the_United_States_Congress" title="Attending Physician of the United States Congress">Office of the Attending Physician</a> at the U.S. Capitol, which current members may seek health care from for an annual fee.<sup id="cite_ref-Mach_&amp;_Cornell_33-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mach_&amp;_Cornell-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The attending physician provides routine exams, consultations, and certain diagnostics, and may write prescriptions (although it does not dispense them).<sup id="cite_ref-Mach_&amp;_Cornell_33-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mach_&amp;_Cornell-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The office does not provide vision or dental care.<sup id="cite_ref-Mach_&amp;_Cornell_33-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mach_&amp;_Cornell-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Current members (but not their dependents, and not former members) may also receive medical and emergency dental care at military treatment facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-Mach_&amp;_Cornell_33-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mach_&amp;_Cornell-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> There is no charge for outpatient care if it is provided in the <a href="/wiki/Washington_metropolitan_area" title="Washington metropolitan area">National Capital Region</a>, but members are billed at full reimbursement rates (set by the Department of Defense) for inpatient care.<sup id="cite_ref-Mach_&amp;_Cornell_33-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mach_&amp;_Cornell-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> (Outside the National Capital Region, charges are at full reimbursement rates for both inpatient and outpatient care).<sup id="cite_ref-Mach_&amp;_Cornell_33-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mach_&amp;_Cornell-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Personnel.2C_mail_and_office_expenses"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Personnel,_mail_and_office_expenses">Personnel, mail and office expenses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Personnel, mail and office expenses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>House members are eligible for a Member's Representational Allowance (MRA) to support them in their official and representational duties to their district.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The MRA is calculated based on three components: one for personnel, one for official office expenses and one for official or franked mail. The personnel allowance is the same for all members; the office and mail allowances vary based on the members' district's distance from Washington, D.C., the cost of office space in the member's district, and the number of non-business addresses in their district. These three components are used to calculate a single MRA that can fund any expenseeven though each component is calculated individually, the franking allowance can be used to pay for personnel expenses if the member so chooses. In 2011 this allowance averaged $1.4 million per member, and ranged from $1.35 to $1.67 million.<sup id="cite_ref-Salaries_and_Allowances_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Salaries_and_Allowances-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Personnel allowance was $944,671 per member in 2010. Each member may employ no more than 18 permanent employees. Members' employees' salary is capped at $168,411 as of 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-Salaries_and_Allowances_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Salaries_and_Allowances-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Travel_allowance">Travel allowance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Travel allowance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Each member-elect and one staffer can be paid for one round trip between their home in their congressional district and Washington, D.C. for organization caucuses.<sup id="cite_ref-Salaries_and_Allowances_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Salaries_and_Allowances-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Officers">Officers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Officers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Member_officials">Member officials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Member officials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/List_of_political_parties_in_the_United_States" title="List of political parties in the United States">party</a> with a majority of seats in the House is known as the <a href="/wiki/Majority_party" class="mw-redirect" title="Majority party">majority party</a>. The next-largest party is the minority party. The <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the United States House of Representatives">speaker</a>, committee chairs, and some other officials are generally from the majority party; they have counterparts (for instance, the "ranking members" of committees) in the minority party.
</p><p>The Constitution provides that the House may choose its own speaker.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Although not explicitly required by the Constitution, every speaker has been a member of the House. The Constitution does not specify the duties and powers of the speaker, which are instead regulated by the rules and customs of the House. Speakers have a role both as a leader of the House and the leader of their party (which need not be the majority party; theoretically, a member of the <a href="/wiki/Minority_party" class="mw-redirect" title="Minority party">minority party</a> could be elected as speaker with the support of a fraction of members of the majority party). Under the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Succession_Act" title="Presidential Succession Act">Presidential Succession Act</a> (1947), the speaker is second in the line of <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_line_of_succession" title="United States presidential line of succession">presidential succession</a> behind the vice president.
</p><p>The speaker is the presiding officer of the House but does not preside over every debate. Instead, s/he delegates the responsibility of presiding to other members in most cases. The presiding officer sits in a chair in the front of the House chamber. The powers of the presiding officer are extensive; one important power is that of controlling the order in which members of the House speak. No member may make a speech or a motion unless s/he has first been recognized by the presiding officer. Moreover, the presiding officer may rule on a "<a href="/wiki/Point_of_order" title="Point of order">point of order</a>" (a member's objection that a rule has been breached); the decision is subject to appeal to the whole House.
</p><p>Speakers serve as chairs of their party's steering committee, which is responsible for assigning party members to other House committees. The speaker chooses the chairmen of standing committees, appoints most of the members of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Rules" title="United States House Committee on Rules">Rules Committee</a>, appoints all members of conference committees, and determines which committees consider bills.
</p><p>Each party elects a <a href="/wiki/Floor_leader" title="Floor leader">floor leader</a>, who is known as the <a href="/wiki/Majority_Leader" class="mw-redirect" title="Majority Leader">majority leader</a> or <a href="/wiki/Minority_Leader" class="mw-redirect" title="Minority Leader">minority leader</a>. The minority leader heads their party in the House, and the majority leader is their party's second-highest-ranking official, behind the speaker. Party leaders decide what legislation members of their party should either support or oppose.
</p><p>Each party also elects a <a href="/wiki/Whip_(politics)" title="Whip (politics)">Whip</a>, who works to ensure that the party's members vote as the party leadership desires. The <a href="/wiki/Assistant_Party_Leaders_of_the_United_States_House" class="mw-redirect" title="Assistant Party Leaders of the United States House">current majority whip</a> in the House of Representatives is <a href="/wiki/Jim_Clyburn" title="Jim Clyburn">Jim Clyburn</a>, who is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Assistant_Party_Leaders_of_the_United_States_House" class="mw-redirect" title="Assistant Party Leaders of the United States House">current minority whip</a> is <a href="/wiki/Steve_Scalise" title="Steve Scalise">Steve Scalise</a>, who is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a>. The whip is supported by <a href="/wiki/Chief_Deputy_Whips_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" class="mw-redirect" title="Chief Deputy Whips of the United States House of Representatives">chief deputy whips</a>.
</p><p>After the whips, the next ranking official in the House party's leadership is the <a href="/wiki/Party_caucuses_and_conferences_in_the_United_States_Congress" title="Party caucuses and conferences in the United States Congress">party conference chair</a> (styled as the Republican conference chair and Democratic caucus chair).
</p><p>After the conference chair, there are differences between each party's subsequent leadership ranks. After the Democratic caucus chair is the campaign committee chair (<a href="/wiki/Democratic_Congressional_Campaign_Committee" title="Democratic Congressional Campaign Committee">Democratic Congressional Campaign Committee</a>), then the co-chairs of the Steering Committee. For the Republicans it is the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Policy_Committee_Chairman_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" class="mw-redirect" title="Republican Policy Committee Chairman of the United States House of Representatives">chair of the House Republican Policy Committee</a>, followed by the campaign committee chairman (styled as the <a href="/wiki/National_Republican_Congressional_Committee" title="National Republican Congressional Committee">National Republican Congressional Committee</a>).
</p><p>The chairs of <a href="/wiki/Standing_committee_(United_States_Congress)" title="Standing committee (United States Congress)">House committees</a>, particularly influential standing committees such as <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Appropriations" title="United States House Committee on Appropriations">Appropriations</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Ways_and_Means" title="United States House Committee on Ways and Means">Ways and Means</a>, and <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Rules" title="United States House Committee on Rules">Rules</a>, are powerful but not officially part of House leadership hierarchy. Until the post of majority leader was created, the chair of Ways and Means was the <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i> majority leader.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Leadership_and_partisanship">Leadership and partisanship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Leadership and partisanship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>When the presidency and Senate are controlled by a different party from the one controlling the House, the speaker can become the <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i> "leader of the opposition". Some notable examples include <a href="/wiki/Tip_O%27Neill" title="Tip O&#39;Neill">Tip O'Neill</a> in the 1980s, <a href="/wiki/Newt_Gingrich" title="Newt Gingrich">Newt Gingrich</a> in the 1990s, <a href="/wiki/John_Boehner" title="John Boehner">John Boehner</a> in the early 2010s, and <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Pelosi" title="Nancy Pelosi">Nancy Pelosi</a> in the late 2000s and again in the late 2010s. Since the speaker is a partisan officer with substantial power to control the business of the House, the position is often used for partisan advantage.
</p><p>In the instance when the presidency and both Houses of Congress are controlled by one party, the speaker normally takes a low profile and defers to the president. For that situation the House minority leader can play the role of a <i>de facto</i> "leader of the opposition", often more so than the Senate minority leader, due to the more partisan nature of the House and the greater role of leadership.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-member_officials">Non-member officials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Non-member officials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The House is also served by several officials who are not members. The House's chief such officer is the <a href="/wiki/Clerk_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Clerk of the United States House of Representatives">clerk</a>, who maintains public records, prepares documents, and oversees junior officials, including <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives_Page" class="mw-redirect" title="United States House of Representatives Page">pages</a> until the discontinuation of House pages in 2011. The clerk also presides over the House at the beginning of each new Congress pending the election of a speaker. Another officer is the <a href="/wiki/Chief_Administrative_Officer_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Chief Administrative Officer of the United States House of Representatives">chief administrative officer</a>, responsible for the day-to-day administrative support to the House of Representatives. This includes everything from payroll to <a href="/wiki/Foodservice" title="Foodservice">foodservice</a>.
</p><p>The position of <a href="/wiki/Chief_Administrative_Officer_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Chief Administrative Officer of the United States House of Representatives">chief administrative officer</a> (CAO) was created by the <a href="/wiki/104th_Congress" class="mw-redirect" title="104th Congress">104th Congress</a> following the <a href="/wiki/United_States_elections,_1994" class="mw-redirect" title="United States elections, 1994">1994 mid-term elections</a>, replacing the positions of <a href="/wiki/Doorkeeper_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Doorkeeper of the United States House of Representatives">doorkeeper</a> and director of non-legislative and financial services (created by the previous congress to administer the non-partisan functions of the House). The CAO also assumed some of the responsibilities of the House Information Services, which previously had been controlled directly by the <a href="/wiki/Committee_on_House_Administration" class="mw-redirect" title="Committee on House Administration">Committee on House Administration</a>, then headed by Representative <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Rose_(congressman)" class="mw-redirect" title="Charlie Rose (congressman)">Charlie Rose</a> of North Carolina, along with the House "Folding Room".
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Chaplain_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Chaplain of the United States House of Representatives">chaplain</a> leads the House in <a href="/wiki/Prayer" title="Prayer">prayer</a> at the opening of the day. There is also a <a href="/wiki/Sergeant_at_Arms_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Sergeant at Arms of the United States House of Representatives">sergeant at arms</a>, who as the House's chief law enforcement officer maintains order and security on House premises. Finally, routine police work is handled by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol_Police" title="United States Capitol Police">United States Capitol Police</a>, which is supervised by the <a href="/wiki/Capitol_Police_Board" title="Capitol Police Board">Capitol Police Board</a>, a body to which the sergeant at arms belongs, and chairs in even-numbered years.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Procedure">Procedure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Procedure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Procedures_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Procedures of the United States House of Representatives">Procedures of the United States House of Representatives</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Daily_procedures">Daily procedures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Daily procedures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Like the Senate, the House of Representatives meets in the United States Capitol in Washington, D.C. At one end of the chamber of the House is a <a href="/wiki/Podium" title="Podium">rostrum</a> from which the <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the United States House of Representatives">speaker</a>, Speaker pro tempore, or (when in the Committee of the Whole) the chair presides.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The lower tier of the rostrum is used by clerks and other officials. Members' seats are arranged in the chamber in a semicircular pattern facing the rostrum and are divided by a wide central aisle.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> By tradition, Democrats sit on the left of the center aisle, while Republicans sit on the right, facing the presiding officer's chair.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Sittings are normally held on weekdays; meetings on Saturdays and Sundays are rare. Sittings of the House are generally open to the public; visitors must obtain a House Gallery pass from a congressional office.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Sittings are broadcast live on television and have been streamed live on <a href="/wiki/C-SPAN" title="C-SPAN">C-SPAN</a> since March 19, 1979,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> and on <i>HouseLive</i>, the official streaming service operated by the Clerk, since the early 2010s.
</p><p>The procedure of the House depends not only on the rules, but also on a variety of customs, precedents, and traditions. In many cases, the House waives some of its stricter rules (including time limits on debates) by <a href="/wiki/Unanimous_consent" title="Unanimous consent">unanimous consent</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> A member may block a unanimous consent agreement; in practice, objections are rare. The presiding officer, the <a href="/wiki/Speaker_(politics)" title="Speaker (politics)">speaker of the House</a> enforces the rules of the House, and may warn members who deviate from them. The speaker uses a <a href="/wiki/Gavel" title="Gavel">gavel</a> to maintain order.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The box in which legislation is placed to be considered by the House is called the <a href="/wiki/Clerk_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Clerk of the United States House of Representatives">hopper</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In one of its first resolutions, the U.S. House of Representatives established the <a href="/wiki/Sergeant_at_Arms_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Sergeant at Arms of the United States House of Representatives">Office of the Sergeant at Arms</a>. In an American tradition adopted from English custom in 1789 by the first speaker of the House, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Muhlenberg" title="Frederick Muhlenberg">Frederick Muhlenberg</a> of <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mace_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Mace of the United States House of Representatives">Mace of the United States House of Representatives</a> is used to open all sessions of the House. It is also used during the inaugural ceremonies for all presidents of the United States. For daily sessions of the House, the sergeant at arms carries the mace in front of the speaker in procession to the <a href="/wiki/Podium" title="Podium">rostrum</a>. It is placed on a green marble pedestal to the speaker's right. When the House is in committee, the mace is moved to a pedestal next to the desk of the Sergeant at Arms.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Constitution provides that a majority of the House constitutes a <a href="/wiki/Quorum" title="Quorum">quorum</a> to do business.<sup id="cite_ref-annotate_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-annotate-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Under the rules and customs of the House, a quorum is always assumed present unless a <a href="/wiki/Quorum_call" title="Quorum call">quorum call</a> explicitly demonstrates otherwise. House rules prevent a member from making a point of order that a quorum is not present unless a question is being voted on. The presiding officer does not accept a point of order of no quorum during general debate, or when a question is not before the House.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During debates, a member may speak only if called upon by the presiding officer. The presiding officer decides which members to recognize, and can therefore control the course of debate.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> All speeches must be addressed to the presiding officer, using the words "Mr. Speaker" or "Madam Speaker". Only the presiding officer may be directly addressed in speeches; other members must be referred to in the third person. In most cases, members do not refer to each other only by name, but also by state, using forms such as "the gentleman from Virginia", "the distinguished gentlewoman from California", or "my distinguished friend from Alabama".
</p><p>There are 448 permanent seats on the House Floor and four tables, two on each side. These tables are occupied by members of the committee that have brought a bill to the floor for consideration and by the respective party leadership. Members address the House from microphones at any table or "the well," the area immediately in front of the rostrum.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Passage_of_legislation">Passage of legislation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Passage of legislation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Per the Constitution, the House of Representatives determines the rules according to which it passes legislation. The rules are in principle open to change with each new Congress, but in practice each new session amends a standing set of rules built up over the history of the body in an early resolution published for public inspection.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Before legislation reaches the floor of the House, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Rules" title="United States House Committee on Rules">Rules Committee</a> normally passes a rule to govern debate on that measure (which then must be passed by the full House before it becomes effective). For instance, the committee determines if amendments to the bill are permitted. An "open rule" permits all germane amendments, but a "closed rule" restricts or even prohibits amendment. Debate on a bill is generally restricted to one hour, equally divided between the majority and minority parties. Each side is led during the debate by a "floor manager", who allocates debate time to members who wish to speak. On contentious matters, many members may wish to speak; thus, a member may receive as little as one minute, or even thirty seconds, to make his/her point.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When debate concludes, the motion in question is put to a vote.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> In many cases, the House votes by voice vote; the presiding officer puts the question, and members respond either "yea" or "aye" (in favor of the motion) or "nay" or "no" (against the motion). The presiding officer then announces the result of the voice vote. A member may however challenge the presiding officer's assessment and "request the yeas and nays" or "request a recorded vote". The request may be granted only if it is seconded by one-fifth of the members present. In practice, however, members of congress second requests for recorded votes as a matter of courtesy. Some votes are always recorded, such as those on the annual budget.<sup id="cite_ref-gpovote_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gpovote-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A recorded vote may be taken in one of three different ways. One is electronically. Members use a personal identification card to record their votes at 46 voting stations in the chamber. Votes are usually held in this way. A second mode of recorded vote is by teller. Members hand in colored cards to indicate their votes: green for "yea", red for "nay", and orange for "present" (i.e., to abstain). Teller votes are normally held only when electronic voting breaks down. Finally, the House may conduct a <a href="/wiki/Roll_call_vote" class="mw-redirect" title="Roll call vote">roll call vote</a>. The Clerk reads the list of members of the House, each of whom announces their vote when their name is called. This procedure is only used rarely (such as for the election of a speaker) because of the time consumed by calling over four hundred names.<sup id="cite_ref-gpovote_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gpovote-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Voting traditionally lasts for, at most, fifteen minutes, but it may be extended if the leadership needs to "whip" more members into alignment.<sup id="cite_ref-gpovote_53-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gpovote-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The 2003 vote on the prescription drug benefit was open for three hours, from 3:00 to 6:00&#160;a.m., to receive four additional votes, three of which were necessary to pass the legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The 2005 vote on the <a href="/wiki/Central_American_Free_Trade_Agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="Central American Free Trade Agreement">Central American Free Trade Agreement</a> was open for one hour, from 11:00&#160;p.m. to midnight.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> An October 2005 vote on facilitating refinery construction was kept open for forty minutes.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Presiding officers may vote like other members. They may not, however, vote twice in the event of a tie; rather, a tie vote defeats the motion.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><br />
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Committees">Committees</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Committees">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/United_States_congressional_committee" title="United States congressional committee">United States congressional committee</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_House_of_Representatives_committees" title="List of United States House of Representatives committees">List of United States House of Representatives committees</a></div>
<p>The House uses committees and their subcommittees for a variety of purposes, including the review of bills and the oversight of the executive branch. The appointment of committee members is formally made by the whole House, but the choice of members is actually made by the political parties. Generally, each party honors the preferences of individual members, giving priority on the basis of seniority. Historically, membership on committees has been in rough proportion to the party's strength in the House as a whole, with two exceptions: on the Rules Committee, the majority party fills nine of the thirteen seats;<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> and on the Ethics Committee, each party has an equal number of seats.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> However, when party control in the House is closely divided, extra seats on committees are sometimes allocated to the majority party. In the 109th Congress, for example, the Republicans controlled about 53% of the House as a whole, but had 54% of the Appropriations Committee members, 55% of the members on the Energy and Commerce Committee, 58% of the members on the Judiciary Committee, and 69% of the members on the Rules Committee.
</p><p>The largest committee of the House is the <a href="/wiki/Committee_of_the_Whole_(United_States_House_of_Representatives)" title="Committee of the Whole (United States House of Representatives)">Committee of the Whole</a>, which, as its name suggests, consists of all members of the House. The Committee meets in the House chamber; it may consider and amend bills, but may not grant them final passage. Generally, the debate procedures of the Committee of the Whole are more flexible than those of the House itself. One advantage of the Committee of the Whole is its ability to include otherwise non-voting members of <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress</a>.
</p><p>Most committee work is performed by twenty standing committees, each of which has jurisdiction over a specific set of issues, such as Agriculture or Foreign Affairs. Each standing committee considers, amends, and reports bills that fall under its jurisdiction. Committees have extensive powers with regard to bills; they may block legislation from reaching the floor of the House. Standing committees also oversee the departments and agencies of the executive branch. In discharging their duties, standing committees have the power to hold hearings and to <a href="/wiki/Subpoena" title="Subpoena">subpoena</a> witnesses and evidence.
</p><p>The House also has one permanent committee that is not a standing committee, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Permanent_Select_Committee_on_Intelligence" title="United States House Permanent Select Committee on Intelligence">Permanent Select Committee on Intelligence</a>, and from time to time may establish committees that are temporary and advisory in nature, such as the <a href="/wiki/Select_Committee_on_Energy_Independence_and_Global_Warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Select Committee on Energy Independence and Global Warming">Select Committee on Energy Independence and Global Warming</a>. This latter committee, created in the 110th Congress and reauthorized for the 111th, has no jurisdiction over legislation and must be chartered anew at the start of every Congress. The House also appoints members to serve on joint committees, which include members of the Senate and House. Some joint committees oversee independent government bodies; for instance, the Joint Committee on the Library oversees the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a>. Other joint committees serve to make advisory reports; for example, there exists a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress_Joint_Committee_on_Taxation" title="United States Congress Joint Committee on Taxation">Joint Committee on Taxation</a>. Bills and nominees are not referred to joint committees. Hence, the power of joint committees is considerably lower than those of standing committees.
</p><p>
Each House committee and subcommittee is led by a chairman (always a member of the majority party). From 1910 to the 1970s, committee chairs were powerful. <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a> in his classic study,<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> suggested:</p><blockquote><p>Power is nowhere concentrated; it is rather deliberately and of set policy scattered amongst many small chiefs. It is divided up, as it were, into forty-seven seigniories, in each of which a Standing Committee is the court-baron and its chairman lord-proprietor. These petty barons, some of them not a little powerful, but none of them within the reach of the full powers of rule, may at will exercise almost despotic sway within their own shires, and may sometimes threaten to convulse even the realm itself.</p></blockquote>
<p>From 1910 to 1975 committee and subcommittee chairmanship was determined purely by seniority; congressmembers sometimes had to wait 30 years to get one, but their chairship was independent of party leadership. The rules were changed in 1975 to permit party <a href="/wiki/Caucus" title="Caucus">caucuses</a> to elect chairmen, shifting power upward to the party leaders. In 1995, Republicans under <a href="/wiki/Newt_Gingrich" title="Newt Gingrich">Newt Gingrich</a> set a limit of three two-year terms for committee chairs. The chairman's powers are extensive; he controls the committee/subcommittee agenda, and may prevent the committee from dealing with a bill. The senior member of the minority party is known as the Ranking Member. In some committees like Appropriations, partisan disputes are few.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legislative_functions">Legislative functions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Legislative functions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Most bills may be introduced in either House of Congress. However, the Constitution states, "All Bills for raising Revenue shall originate in the House of Representatives". As a result of the <a href="/wiki/Origination_Clause" title="Origination Clause">Origination Clause</a>, the Senate cannot initiate bills imposing taxes. This provision barring the Senate from introducing revenue bills is based on the practice of the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">British Parliament</a>, in which only the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a> may originate such measures. Furthermore, congressional tradition holds that the House of Representatives originates <a href="/wiki/Appropriation_bill" title="Appropriation bill">appropriation bills</a>.
</p><p>Although it cannot originate revenue bills, the Senate retains the power to amend or reject them. <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a> wrote the following about appropriations bills:<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>[T]he constitutional prerogative of the House has been held to apply to all the general appropriations bills, and the Senate's right to amend these has been allowed the widest possible scope. The upper house may add to them what it pleases; may go altogether outside of their original provisions and tack to them entirely new features of legislation, altering not only the amounts but even the objects of expenditure, and making out of the materials sent them by the popular chamber measures of an almost totally new character.</p></blockquote>
<p>The approval of the Senate and the House of Representatives is required for a bill to become law. Both Houses must pass the same version of the bill; if there are differences, they may be resolved by a <a href="/wiki/Conference_committee" class="mw-redirect" title="Conference committee">conference committee</a>, which includes members of both bodies. For the stages through which bills pass in the Senate, see <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Congress" title="Act of Congress">Act of Congress</a>.
</p><p>The president may <a href="/wiki/Veto" title="Veto">veto</a> a bill passed by the House and Senate. If he does, the bill does not become law unless each House, by a two-thirds vote, votes to override the veto.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Checks_and_balances">Checks and balances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Checks and balances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Constitution provides that the Senate's "<a href="/wiki/Advice_and_consent" title="Advice and consent">advice and consent</a>" is necessary for the president to make appointments and to ratify treaties. Thus, with its potential to frustrate presidential appointments, the Senate is more powerful than the House.
</p><p>The Constitution empowers the House of Representatives to <a href="/wiki/Impeachment" title="Impeachment">impeach</a> federal officials for "<a href="/wiki/Treason" title="Treason">Treason</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bribery" title="Bribery">Bribery</a>, or other high <a href="/wiki/Crime" title="Crime">Crimes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Misdemeanor" title="Misdemeanor">Misdemeanors</a>" and empowers the Senate to try such impeachments. The House may approve "articles of impeachment" by a simple majority vote; however, a two-thirds vote is required for conviction in the Senate. A convicted official is automatically removed from office and may be disqualified from holding future office under the United States. No further punishment is permitted during the impeachment proceedings; however, the party may face criminal penalties in a normal court of law.
</p><p>In the history of the United States, the House of Representatives has impeached sixteen officials, of whom seven were convicted. (Another, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a>, resigned after the <a href="/wiki/House_Judiciary_Committee" class="mw-redirect" title="House Judiciary Committee">House Judiciary Committee</a> passed <a href="/wiki/Articles_of_impeachment" title="Articles of impeachment">articles of impeachment</a> but before a formal impeachment vote by the full House.) Only two presidents of the United States have ever been impeached: <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Johnson" title="Andrew Johnson">Andrew Johnson</a> in 1868 and <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a> in 1998. Both trials ended in acquittal; in Johnson's case, the Senate fell one vote short of the two-thirds majority required for conviction.
</p><p>Under the <a href="/wiki/Twelfth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twelfth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Twelfth Amendment</a>, the House has the power to elect the president if no presidential candidate receives a majority of votes in the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="Electoral College (United States)">Electoral College</a>. The Twelfth Amendment requires the House to choose from the three candidates with the highest numbers of electoral votes. The Constitution provides that "the votes shall be taken by states, the representation from each state having one vote." It is rare for no presidential candidate to receive a majority of electoral votes. In the history of the United States, the House has only had to choose a president twice. In 1800, which was before the adoption of the <a href="/wiki/Twelfth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twelfth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Twelfth Amendment</a>, it elected <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> over <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Burr" title="Aaron Burr">Aaron Burr</a>. In 1824, it elected <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a> over <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_H._Crawford" title="William H. Crawford">William H. Crawford</a>. (If no vice-presidential candidate receives a majority of the electoral votes, the Senate elects the vice president from the two candidates with the highest numbers of electoral votes.)
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Plans-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Plans_6-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Plans_6-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Plans_6-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://history.house.gov/People/Signatories/Signatories/">"Delegates of the Continental Congress Who Signed the United States Constitution"</a>, United States House of Representatives. Accessed February 19, 2017. "While some believed the Articles should be 'corrected and enlarged as to accomplish the objects proposed by their institution,' the Virginia Plan called for completely replacing it with a strong central government based on popular consent and proportional representation.... The Virginia Plan received support from states with large populations such as Pennsylvania, Massachusetts, and South Carolina. A number of smaller states, however, proposed the 'New Jersey Plan,' drafted by William Paterson, which retained the essential features of the original Articles: a unicameral legislature where all states had equal representation, the appointment of a plural executive, and a supreme court of limited jurisdiction.... The committees report, dubbed the Great Compromise, ironed out many contentious points. It resolved the delegates sharpest disagreement by prescribing a bicameral legislature with proportional representation in the House and equal state representation in the Senate. After two more months of intense debates and revisions, the delegates produced the document we now know as the Constitution, which expanded the power of the central government while protecting the prerogatives of the states."</span>

<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.congress.gov/bill/110th-congress/house-resolution/78">H.Res. 78</a>, passed January 24, 2007. On April 19, 2007, the House of Representatives passed the DC House Voting Rights Act of 2007, a bill "to provide for the treatment of the District of Columbia as a Congressional district for purposes of representation in the House of Representatives, and for other purposes" by a vote of 241177. That bill proposes to increase the House membership by two, making 437 members, by converting the District of Columbia delegate into a member, and (until the 2010 census) grant one membership to <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a>, which is the state next in line to receive an additional district based on its population after the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census,_2000" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Census, 2000">2000 Census</a>. The bill was under consideration in the U.S. Senate during the 2007 session.</span>

Archived from <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://lawreview.richmond.edu/where-do-we-draw-the-line-partisan-gerrymandering-and-the-state-of-texas/">the original</a> on October 9, 2013.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=University+of+Richmond+Law+Review&amp;rft.atitle=Where+Do+We+Draw+the+Line%3F+Partisan+Gerrymandering+and+the+State+of+Texas&amp;rft.date=2006-05&amp;rft.aulast=Eaton&amp;rft.aufirst=Whitney+M.&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Flawreview.richmond.edu%2Fwhere-do-we-draw-the-line-partisan-gerrymandering-and-the-state-of-texas%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AUnited+States+House+of+Representatives" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_House_of_Representatives&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
